## Species

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Crocodylidae_Distribution.png/960px-Crocodylidae_Distribution.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Crocodylidae_Distribution.png)

Distribution of crocodiles

A total of 18 [extant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extant_taxon "Extant taxon") species have been recognized. Further [genetic study](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genetic_study "Genetic study") is needed for the confirmation of proposed species under the genus _[Osteolaemus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Osteolaemus "Osteolaemus")_.

| Species name | Image | Distribution | Description/Comments |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| [American crocodile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_crocodile "American crocodile") (_Crocodylus acutus_) | [![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Crocodylus_acutus_mexico_02-edit1.jpg/250px-Crocodylus_acutus_mexico_02-edit1.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Crocodylus_acutus_mexico_02-edit1.jpg) | Throughout the [Caribbean Basin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Caribbean_Basin "Caribbean Basin"), including many of the [Caribbean islands](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Caribbean_island "Caribbean island") and [South Florida](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_Florida "South Florida"). | A larger sized species, with a greyish colour and a prominent V-shaped snout. Prefers [brackish](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brackish "Brackish") water, but also inhabits lower stretches of [rivers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/River "River") and true [marine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marine_biology "Marine biology") environments. This is one of the rare species that exhibits regular sea-going behaviour, which explains the great distribution throughout the [Caribbean](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Caribbean "Caribbean"). It is also found in [hypersaline lakes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hypersaline_lake "Hypersaline lake") such as [Lago Enriquillo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lago_Enriquillo "Lago Enriquillo"), in the [Dominican Republic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dominican_Republic "Dominican Republic"), which has one of the largest populations of this species.[\[45\]](#cite_note-nationalgeographic1-51) Diet consists mostly of aquatic and terrestrial vertebrates. Classified as [Vulnerable](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vulnerable_species "Vulnerable species"), but certain local populations under greater threat. |
| [Hall's New Guinea crocodile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crocodylus_halli "Crocodylus halli") (_Crocodylus halli_) |  | The island of [New Guinea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Guinea "New Guinea"), south of the [New Guinea Highlands](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Guinea_Highlands "New Guinea Highlands") | A smaller species that closely resembles and was long classified under the [New Guinea crocodile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Guinea_crocodile "New Guinea crocodile"), which it is now considered to be genetically distinct from. It lives south of the mountain barrier that divides the two species' ranges. It can be physically distinguished from the New Guinea crocodile by its shorter [maxilla](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maxilla "Maxilla") and enlarged [postcranial](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Postcrania "Postcrania") elements. Cranial elements can still widely vary within the species, with populations from [Lake Murray](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lake_Murray_\(Papua_New_Guinea\) "Lake Murray (Papua New Guinea)") having much wider heads than those from the [Aramia River](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Aramia_River_\(New_Guinea\)&action=edit&redlink=1 "Aramia River (New Guinea) (page does not exist)").[\[46\]](#cite_note-52) |
| [Orinoco crocodile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orinoco_crocodile "Orinoco crocodile") (_Crocodylus intermedius_) | [![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Croc_inter.jpg/250px-Croc_inter.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Croc_inter.jpg) | [Colombia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Colombia "Colombia") and [Venezuela](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Venezuela "Venezuela") | This is a large species with a relatively elongated snout and a pale tan coloration with scattered dark brown markings. Lives primarily in the [Orinoco Basin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orinoco "Orinoco"). Despite having a rather narrow snout, preys on a wide variety of vertebrates, including large mammals. It is a [Critically Endangered](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Critically_Endangered "Critically Endangered") species. |
| [Freshwater crocodile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Freshwater_crocodile "Freshwater crocodile") (_Crocodylus johnstoni_) | [![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Australia_Cairns_18.jpg/250px-Australia_Cairns_18.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Australia_Cairns_18.jpg) | [Northern Australia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Northern_Australia "Northern Australia") | A smaller species with a narrow and elongated snout. It has light brown coloration with darker bands on body and tail. Lives in [rivers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/River "River") with considerable distance from the sea, to avoid confrontations with saltwater crocodiles. Feeds mostly on [fish](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fish "Fish") and other small vertebrates. |
| [Philippine crocodile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Philippine_crocodile "Philippine crocodile") (_Crocodylus mindorensis_) | [![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Crocodylus_mindorensis_basking_on_a_rock_in_the_Disulap_River%2C_Barangay_Disulap_-_ZooKeys-266-001-g102.jpg/250px-Crocodylus_mindorensis_basking_on_a_rock_in_the_Disulap_River%2C_Barangay_Disulap_-_ZooKeys-266-001-g102.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Crocodylus_mindorensis_basking_on_a_rock_in_the_Disulap_River,_Barangay_Disulap_-_ZooKeys-266-001-g102.jpg) | [Endemic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Endemic "Endemic") to the [Philippines](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Philippines "Philippines") | This is a relatively small species with a rather broader snout. It has heavy dorsal armour and a golden-brown colour that darkens as the animal matures. Prefers [freshwater](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Freshwater "Freshwater") habitats and feeds on a variety of small to medium sized vertebrates. This species is [Critically Endangered](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Critically_Endangered "Critically Endangered") and the most severely threatened species of crocodile.[\[47\]](#cite_note-53) |
| [Morelet's crocodile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Morelet%27s_crocodile "Morelet's crocodile") (_Crocodylus moreletii_) | [![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Morelets.crocodile.arp.jpg/250px-Morelets.crocodile.arp.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Morelets.crocodile.arp.jpg) | Atlantic regions of [Mexico](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mexico "Mexico"), [Belize](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Belize "Belize") and [Guatemala](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Guatemala "Guatemala") | A small to medium sized crocodile with a rather broad snout. It has a dark greyish-brown colour and is found in mostly various [freshwater](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Freshwater "Freshwater") habitats. Feeds on mammals, birds and reptiles. It is listed as [Least Concern](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Least_Concern "Least Concern"). |
| [Nile crocodile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nile_crocodile "Nile crocodile") (_Crocodylus niloticus_) | [![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Nile_croc_couple_690V1510_-_Flickr_-_Lip_Kee.jpg/250px-Nile_croc_couple_690V1510_-_Flickr_-_Lip_Kee.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Nile_croc_couple_690V1510_-_Flickr_-_Lip_Kee.jpg) | [Sub-Saharan Africa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa "Sub-Saharan Africa") | A large and aggressive species with a broad snout, especially in older animals. It has a dark bronze coloration and darkens as the animal matures. Lives in a variety of freshwater habitats but is also found in brackish water. It is an [apex predator](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apex_predator "Apex predator") that is capable of taking a wide array of [African](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Africa "Africa") vertebrates, including large ungulates and other predators.[\[48\]](#cite_note-54) This species is listed as [Least Concern](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Least_Concern "Least Concern"). |
| [New Guinea crocodile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Guinea_crocodile "New Guinea crocodile") (_Crocodylus novaeguineae_) | [![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Neuguinea-krokodil-0272.jpg/250px-Neuguinea-krokodil-0272.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Neuguinea-krokodil-0272.jpg) | The island of [New Guinea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Guinea "New Guinea"), north of the [New Guinea Highlands](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Guinea_Highlands "New Guinea Highlands") | A smaller species of crocodile with a grey-brown colour and dark brown to black markings on the tail. The young have a narrower V-shaped snout that becomes wider as the animal matures. Prefers [freshwater](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Freshwater "Freshwater") habitats, even though is tolerant to salt water, in order to avoid competition and predation by the saltwater crocodile. This species feeds on small to mid-sized vertebrates. |
| [Mugger crocodile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mugger_crocodile "Mugger crocodile") (_Crocodylus palustris_) | [![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Mugger_crocodile_Crocodylus_palustris_%282155269175%29.jpg/250px-Mugger_crocodile_Crocodylus_palustris_%282155269175%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Mugger_crocodile_Crocodylus_palustris_\(2155269175\).jpg) | The [Indian subcontinent](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indian_subcontinent "Indian subcontinent") and surrounding countries | This is a modest sized crocodile with a very broad snout and an alligator-like appearance. It has dark-grey to brown coloration. Enlarged scutes around the neck make it a heavily armoured species. Prefers slow moving [rivers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/River "River"), [swamps](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Swamp "Swamp") and [lakes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lake "Lake"). It can also be found in [coastal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coastal "Coastal") swamps but avoids areas populated by saltwater crocodiles.[\[49\]](#cite_note-55) Feeds on a wide array of vertebrates. |
| [Saltwater crocodile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saltwater_crocodile "Saltwater crocodile") (_Crocodylus porosus_) | [![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Crocodylus_porosus_4.jpg/250px-Crocodylus_porosus_4.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Crocodylus_porosus_4.jpg) | Throughout [Southeast Asia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Southeast_Asia "Southeast Asia"), [Northern Australia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Northern_Australia "Northern Australia") and surrounding waters | The largest on average living reptile and most aggressive of all crocodiles. It is a big-headed species and has a relatively broad snout, especially when older. The coloration is pale yellow with black stripes when young but dark greenish-drab coloured as adults. Lives in [brackish](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brackish "Brackish") and [marine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marine_biology "Marine biology") environments as well as lower stretches of [rivers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/River "River"). This species has the greatest distribution of all crocodiles. Tagged specimens showed long-distance marine travelling behaviour. It is the [apex predator](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apex_predator "Apex predator") throughout its range and preys on virtually any animal within its reach. It is classified as [Least Concern](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Least_Concern "Least Concern") but with several populations under greater risk.[\[50\]](#cite_note-IUCN-56) |
| [Borneo crocodile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crocodylus_raninus "Crocodylus raninus") (_Crocodylus raninus_) |  | Island of Borneo in [Southeast Asia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Southeast_Asia "Southeast Asia") | A freshwater species of crocodile that has been considered a synonym of the [saltwater crocodile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saltwater_crocodile "Saltwater crocodile"). |
| [Cuban crocodile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cuban_crocodile "Cuban crocodile") (_Crocodylus rhombifer_) | [![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Cuban_Crocodile.JPG/250px-Cuban_Crocodile.JPG)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Cuban_Crocodile.JPG) | Found only in the [Zapata Swamp](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zapata_Swamp "Zapata Swamp") and [Isle of Youth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isle_of_Youth "Isle of Youth") of [Cuba](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cuba "Cuba") | It is a small but extremely aggressive species of crocodile that prefers [freshwater](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Freshwater "Freshwater") [swamps](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Swamps "Swamps").[\[51\]](#cite_note-57) The coloration is vibrant even as adults and the scales have a "pebbled" appearance. It is a relatively terrestrial species with agile locomotion on land, and sometimes displays terrestrial hunting. The snout is broad with a thick upper-jaw and large teeth. The unique characteristics and fossil record indicates a rather specialized [diet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diet_\(nutrition\) "Diet (nutrition)") in the past, preying on megafauna such as the giant sloth. This species sometimes displays pack-hunting behaviour, which might have been the key to hunting large species in the past, despite its small size.[\[52\]](#cite_note-Alexander-58) Today most prey are small to medium sized vertebrates. It is [Critically Endangered](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Critically_Endangered "Critically Endangered"), and the remaining wild population is under threat of hybridization.[\[53\]](#cite_note-59) |
| [Siamese crocodile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Siamese_crocodile "Siamese crocodile") (_Crocodylus siamensis_) | [![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Crocodylus_siamensis_%28head%2C_mouth_open%2C_in_zoo%29.jpg/250px-Crocodylus_siamensis_%28head%2C_mouth_open%2C_in_zoo%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Crocodylus_siamensis_\(head,_mouth_open,_in_zoo\).jpg) | [Indonesia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indonesia "Indonesia"), [Brunei](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brunei "Brunei"), [East Malaysia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/East_Malaysia "East Malaysia") and southern [Indochina](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indochina "Indochina") | A fairly small crocodile that prefers [freshwater](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Freshwater "Freshwater") habitats. It has a relatively broad snout and olive-green to dark green coloration. It feeds on a variety of small to mid-sized vertebrates. Listed as [Critically Endangered](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Critically_Endangered "Critically Endangered"), but might be already extinct in the wild; status is unknown.[\[54\]](#cite_note-Bezuijen-60) |
| [West African crocodile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/West_African_crocodile "West African crocodile") (_Crocodylus suchus_) | [![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Crocodile_-_Copenhagen_Zoo.jpg/250px-Crocodile_-_Copenhagen_Zoo.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Crocodile_-_Copenhagen_Zoo.jpg) | [Western](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Western_Africa "Western Africa") and [Central Africa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_Africa "Central Africa") | Recent studies revealed that this is distinct species from the larger Nile crocodile.[\[55\]](#cite_note-61)[\[56\]](#cite_note-schmitz-62) It has a slightly narrower snout and is much smaller compared to its larger cousin. |
| [Osborn's dwarf crocodile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Osborn%27s_dwarf_crocodile "Osborn's dwarf crocodile") (_Osteolaemus osborni_) | [![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Osteolaemus_osborni_88103967.jpg/250px-Osteolaemus_osborni_88103967.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Osteolaemus_osborni_88103967.jpg) | [Western Africa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Western_Africa "Western Africa") | It is a heavily armoured species with uniform black coloration in adults, while juveniles have a lighter brown banding. Lives in the tropical forests of Western Africa. Feeds on small vertebrates and large aquatic invertebrates. It is a fairly terrestrial species and exhibits terrestrial hunting, especially at night. |
| [Dwarf crocodile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dwarf_crocodile "Dwarf crocodile") (_Osteolaemus tetraspis_) | [![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Dwarf_crocodile_01.JPG/250px-Dwarf_crocodile_01.JPG)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Dwarf_crocodile_01.JPG) | [Western Africa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Western_Africa "Western Africa") | It belongs to its own [monotypic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Monotypic "Monotypic") [genus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genus "Genus"); however, new studies indicate there might be two or even three distinct species.[\[36\]](#cite_note-Eaton-42) It is a heavily armoured species with uniform black coloration in adults, while juveniles have a lighter brown banding. Lives in the tropical forests of Western Africa. Feeds on small vertebrates and large aquatic invertebrates. It is a fairly terrestrial species and exhibits terrestrial hunting, especially at night. This species is classified as [Vulnerable](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vulnerable_species "Vulnerable species"). |
| [West African slender-snouted crocodile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/West_African_slender-snouted_crocodile "West African slender-snouted crocodile") (_Mecistops cataphractus_) | [![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Crocodylus_cataphractus_faux-gavial_d%27Afrique2.JPG/250px-Crocodylus_cataphractus_faux-gavial_d%27Afrique2.JPG)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Crocodylus_cataphractus_faux-gavial_d%27Afrique2.JPG) | [Western Africa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Western_Africa "Western Africa") | A medium sized species with a narrow and elongated snout. Lives in [freshwater](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Freshwater "Freshwater") habitats within tropical forests of the continent. Feeds mostly on [fish](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fish "Fish") but also other small to medium sized vertebrates. It is a [Critically Endangered](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Critically_endangered "Critically endangered") species. |
| [Central African slender-snouted crocodile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_African_slender-snouted_crocodile "Central African slender-snouted crocodile") (_Mecistops leptorhynchus_) | [![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Mecistops_leptorhynchus_2009.jpg/250px-Mecistops_leptorhynchus_2009.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Mecistops_leptorhynchus_2009.jpg) | [Central Africa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_Africa "Central Africa") | A medium sized species found in watery areas in dense rainforest. Feeds largely on fish. Insufficient conservation data, but was classified as [Critically Endangered](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Critically_endangered "Critically endangered") when lumped with _M. cataphractus_, although _M. leptorhynchus_ is doing better in its home range. |

For information on Tomistoma or false gharial, that is recently not considered as a true crocodile, see [False gharial](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/False_gharial "False gharial").

## Characteristics

Crocodiles are similar to alligators and caimans; for their common characteristics and differences among them, see [Crocodilia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crocodilia "Crocodilia").

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Skull_%26_scutes_-_Crocodylinae.jpg/250px-Skull_%26_scutes_-_Crocodylinae.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Skull_%26_scutes_-_Crocodylinae.jpg)

Skulls and scutes of [American](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_crocodile "American crocodile"), [Nile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nile_crocodile "Nile crocodile") and [Saltwater crocodiles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saltwater_crocodile "Saltwater crocodile"), with post-occipital [scutes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scute "Scute") highlighted in red, [nuchal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nape "Nape") shield in blue and dorsal scutes in green

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Crocodilelyd5.png/250px-Crocodilelyd5.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Crocodilelyd5.png)

Crocodiles, like dinosaurs, have the abdominal ribs modified into _[gastralia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gastralium "Gastralium")_.

Apart from the advantage conferred by its sufficiently large size relative to other animals in the ecosystem,[\[57\]](#cite_note-63) other physical traits contribute a crocodile's position as [predator](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Predator "Predator"). Its external [morphology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Morphology_\(biology\) "Morphology (biology)") is a sign of its [aquatic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aquatic_animal "Aquatic animal") and predatory lifestyle. Its [streamlined](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Drag_\(physics\) "Drag (physics)") body enables it to swim swiftly; it also tucks its feet to the side while swimming, making it faster by decreasing water resistance. Crocodiles have [webbed feet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Webbed_feet "Webbed feet") which, though not used to propel them through the water, allow them to make fast turns and sudden moves in the water or initiate swimming. Webbed feet are an advantage in shallow water, where the animals sometimes move around by walking. Crocodiles have a [palatal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Palatal "Palatal") flap, a rigid tissue at the back of the mouth that blocks the entry of water. The palate has a special path from the [nostril](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nostril "Nostril") to the [glottis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glottis "Glottis") that bypasses the mouth. The nostrils are closed during submergence.

Like other [archosaurs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archosaur "Archosaur"), crocodilians are [diapsid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diapsid "Diapsid"), although their [post-temporal fenestrae](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Post-temporal_fenestra "Post-temporal fenestra") are reduced. The walls of the braincase are bony but lack supratemporal and postfrontal bones.[\[58\]](#cite_note-ausfauna-64) Their [tongues](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tongue "Tongue") are not free, but held in place by a membrane that limits movement; as a result, crocodiles are unable to stick out their tongues.[\[59\]](#cite_note-65) Crocodiles have smooth skin on their bellies and sides, while their dorsal surfaces are armoured with large [osteoderms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Osteoderms "Osteoderms"). The armoured skin has scales and is thick and rugged, providing some protection. They are still able to absorb heat through this armour, as a network of small [capillaries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Capillaries "Capillaries") allows blood through the scales to absorb heat. The osteoderms are highly vascularised and aid in calcium balance, both to neutralize acids while the animal cannot breathe underwater[\[60\]](#cite_note-66) and to provide calcium for eggshell formation.[\[61\]](#cite_note-:0-67) Crocodilian tegument have pores believed to be sensory in function, analogous to the [lateral line](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lateral_line "Lateral line") in fishes. They are particularly seen on their upper and lower jaws. Another possibility is that they are secretory, as they produce an oily substance which appears to flush mud off.[\[58\]](#cite_note-ausfauna-64)

### Size

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Large_Crocodylus_porosus.jpg/250px-Large_Crocodylus_porosus.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Large_Crocodylus_porosus.jpg)

A [saltwater crocodile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saltwater_crocodile "Saltwater crocodile") in captivity

Size greatly varies among species, from the [dwarf crocodile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dwarf_crocodile "Dwarf crocodile") to the [saltwater crocodile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saltwater_crocodile "Saltwater crocodile"). Species of the dwarf crocodile _Osteolaemus_ grow to an adult size of just 1.5 to 1.9 m (4.9 to 6.2 ft),[\[62\]](#cite_note-68) whereas the saltwater crocodile can grow to sizes over 6 m (20 ft) and weigh over 1,000 kg (2,200 lb).[\[63\]](#cite_note-69) Several other large species can reach over 5.2 m (17 ft) long and weigh over 900 kg (2,000 lb). Crocodilians show pronounced [sexual dimorphism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism "Sexual dimorphism"), with males growing much larger and more rapidly than females.[\[58\]](#cite_note-ausfauna-64) Despite their large adult sizes, crocodiles start their lives at around 20 cm (7.9 in) long. The largest species of crocodile is the saltwater crocodile, found in eastern India, northern Australia, throughout [South-east Asia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South-east_Asia "South-east Asia"), and in the surrounding waters.

The brain volume of two adult crocodiles was 5.6 cm3 for a [spectacled caiman](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spectacled_caiman "Spectacled caiman") and 8.5 cm3 for a larger [Nile crocodile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nile_crocodile "Nile crocodile").[\[64\]](#cite_note-70)

The largest crocodile ever held in captivity is a saltwater–Siamese hybrid named Yai ([Thai](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thai_language "Thai language"): ใหญ่, meaning big; 10 June 1972 – 15 August 2025[\[65\]](#cite_note-71)) at the [Samutprakarn Crocodile Farm and Zoo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Samutprakarn_Crocodile_Farm_and_Zoo "Samutprakarn Crocodile Farm and Zoo"), [Thailand](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thailand "Thailand"). This animal measures 6 m (20 ft) in length and weighs 1,200 kg (2,600 lb).[\[66\]](#cite_note-72)

The longest crocodile captured alive was [Lolong](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lolong "Lolong"), a saltwater crocodile which was measured at 6.17 m (20.2 ft) and weighed at 1,075 kg (2,370 lb) by a National Geographic team in Agusan del Sur Province, Philippines.[\[67\]](#cite_note-73)[\[68\]](#cite_note-74)[\[69\]](#cite_note-75)

### Teeth

Crocodiles are [polyphyodonts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyphyodont "Polyphyodont"); they are able to replace each of their 80 teeth up to 50 times in their 35- to 75-year lifespan.[\[70\]](#cite_note-76)[\[71\]](#cite_note-77) Next to each full-grown tooth, there is a small replacement tooth and an [odontogenic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Animal_tooth_development "Animal tooth development") [stem cell](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stem_cell "Stem cell") in the [dental lamina](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dental_lamina "Dental lamina") in standby that can be activated if required.[\[72\]](#cite_note-78)

## Biology and behaviour

Crocodilians are more closely related to birds and other dinosaurs than to most animals classified as reptiles, the three families being included in the group [Archosauria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archosauria "Archosauria") ('ruling reptiles'). Despite their prehistoric look, crocodiles are among the more biologically complex reptiles. Unlike other reptiles, a crocodile has a [cerebral cortex](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cerebral_cortex "Cerebral cortex") and a four-chambered [heart](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heart "Heart"). Crocodilians also have the functional equivalent of a diaphragm by incorporating muscles used for aquatic locomotion into respiration.[\[73\]](#cite_note-Uriona_&_Farmer,_2008-79) [Salt glands](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Salt_gland "Salt gland") are present in the tongues of crocodiles and they have a pore opening on the surface of the tongue, a trait that separates them from alligators. Salt glands are dysfunctional in Alligatoridae.[\[58\]](#cite_note-ausfauna-64) Their function appears to be similar to that of salt glands in [marine turtles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marine_turtle "Marine turtle"). Crocodiles do not have sweat glands and release heat through their mouths. They often sleep with their mouths open and may pant like a dog.[\[74\]](#cite_note-80) Four species of freshwater crocodile climb trees to bask in areas lacking a shoreline.[\[75\]](#cite_note-81)

### Senses

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/A_crocodiles_eye_%287825799462%29.jpg/250px-A_crocodiles_eye_%287825799462%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:A_crocodiles_eye_\(7825799462\).jpg)

Crocodile eye

Crocodiles have acute senses, an evolutionary advantage that makes them successful predators. The eyes, ears and nostrils are located on top of the head, allowing the crocodile to lie low in the water, almost totally submerged and hidden from prey.

#### Vision

Crocodiles have very good night vision, and are mostly [nocturnal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nocturnality "Nocturnality") hunters. They use the disadvantage of most prey animals' poor nocturnal vision to their advantage. The light receptors in crocodilians' eyes include [cones](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cone_cell "Cone cell") and numerous [rods](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rod_cells "Rod cells"), so it is assumed all crocodilians can see colours.[\[76\]](#cite_note-82) Crocodiles have vertical-slit shaped pupils, similar to those of domestic cats. One explanation for the evolution of slit pupils is that they exclude light more effectively than a circular pupil, helping to protect the eyes during daylight.[\[77\]](#cite_note-Land,_\(2006\)-83) On the rear wall of the eye is a [tapetum lucidum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tapetum_lucidum "Tapetum lucidum"), which reflects incoming light back onto the retina, thus utilizing the small amount of light available at night to best advantage. In addition to the protection of the upper and lower eyelids, crocodiles have a [nictitating membrane](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nictitating_membrane "Nictitating membrane") (sometimes called a "third eyelid") that can be drawn over the eye from the inner corner while the lids are open. The eyeball surface is thus protected under the water while a certain degree of vision is still possible.[\[78\]](#cite_note-Encyclopædia_Britannica-84)

#### Olfaction

Crocodilian [sense of smell](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Olfaction "Olfaction") is also very well developed, aiding them to detect prey or animal carcasses that are either on land or in water, from far away. It is possible that crocodiles use olfaction in the egg prior to hatching.[\[78\]](#cite_note-Encyclopædia_Britannica-84)

[Chemoreception](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chemoreception "Chemoreception") in crocodiles is especially interesting because they hunt in both terrestrial and aquatic surroundings. Crocodiles have only one olfactory chamber and the [vomeronasal organ](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vomeronasal_organ "Vomeronasal organ") is absent in the adults[\[79\]](#cite_note-85) indicating all olfactory perception is limited to the olfactory system. Behavioural and olfactometer experiments indicate that crocodiles detect both air-borne and water-soluble chemicals and use their olfactory system for hunting. When above water, crocodiles enhance their ability to detect volatile odorants by gular pumping, a rhythmic movement of the floor of the pharynx.[\[80\]](#cite_note-86)[\[81\]](#cite_note-87) Crocodiles close their nostrils when submerged, so olfaction underwater is unlikely. Underwater food detection is presumably gustatory and tactile.[\[82\]](#cite_note-88)

#### Hearing

Crocodiles can hear well; their [tympanic membranes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eardrum "Eardrum") are concealed by flat flaps that may be raised or lowered by muscles.[\[58\]](#cite_note-ausfauna-64)

#### Touch

The touch sensors, concentrated in crocodile skin, can be thicker than those in human fingerprints.[\[83\]](#cite_note-89) Crocodiles can feel touch on their skin.[\[84\]](#cite_note-90)

**Cranial**: The upper and lower jaws are covered with sensory pits, visible as small, black speckles on the skin, the crocodilian version of the [lateral line](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lateral_line "Lateral line") organs seen in fish and many amphibians, though arising from a completely different origin. These pigmented nodules encase bundles of [nerve fibers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Axon "Axon") innervated beneath by branches of the trigeminal nerve. They respond to the slightest disturbance in surface water, detecting vibrations and small pressure changes as small as a single drop.[\[85\]](#cite_note-91) This makes it possible for crocodiles to detect prey, danger and intruders, even in total darkness. These sense organs are known as domed pressure receptors (DPRs).[\[86\]](#cite_note-Jackson_and_Brooks-92)

**Post-Cranial**: While alligators and caimans have DPRs only on their jaws, crocodiles have similar organs on almost every scale on their bodies. The function of the DPRs on the jaws is clear; to catch prey, but it is still not clear what the function is of the organs on the rest of the body. The receptors flatten when exposed to increased osmotic pressure, such as that experienced when swimming in sea water [hyperosmotic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hyperosmotic "Hyperosmotic") to the body fluids. When contact between the integument and the surrounding sea water solution is blocked, crocodiles are found to lose their ability to discriminate salinities. It has been proposed that the flattening of the sensory organ in hyperosmotic sea water is sensed by the animal as "touch", but interpreted as chemical information about its surroundings.[\[86\]](#cite_note-Jackson_and_Brooks-92) This might be why in alligators they are absent on the rest of the body.[\[87\]](#cite_note-93)

### Hunting and diet

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Crocodile_attack_during_Mara_River_crossing_-_frame_1_-_Flickr_-_Lip_Kee.jpg/250px-Crocodile_attack_during_Mara_River_crossing_-_frame_1_-_Flickr_-_Lip_Kee.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Crocodile_attack_during_Mara_River_crossing_-_frame_1_-_Flickr_-_Lip_Kee.jpg)

Nile crocodile attacking wildebeest

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Human_Crocodile_Conflict.jpg/250px-Human_Crocodile_Conflict.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Human_Crocodile_Conflict.jpg)

An example of humans and mugger crocodiles in the same environment

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Crocodile_at_Ranganathittu%2C_Mysore%2C_Karnataka.JPG/250px-Crocodile_at_Ranganathittu%2C_Mysore%2C_Karnataka.JPG)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Crocodile_at_Ranganathittu,_Mysore,_Karnataka.JPG)

Even a cruising crocodile is difficult to locate

Crocodiles are [ambush predators](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ambush_predator "Ambush predator"), waiting for fish or land animals to come close, then rushing out to attack. Crocodiles mostly eat [fish](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fish "Fish"), [amphibians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amphibian "Amphibian"), [crustaceans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crustacean "Crustacean"), [molluscs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mollusc "Mollusc"), [birds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bird "Bird"), [reptiles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reptile "Reptile"), and [mammals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mammal "Mammal"), and they occasionally [cannibalize](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cannibalize "Cannibalize") smaller crocodiles. What a crocodile eats varies greatly with species, size and age. From the mostly fish-eating species, like the [slender-snouted](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Slender-snouted_crocodile "Slender-snouted crocodile") and [freshwater crocodiles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Freshwater_crocodile "Freshwater crocodile"), to the larger species like the [Nile crocodile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nile_crocodile "Nile crocodile") and the [saltwater crocodile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saltwater_crocodile "Saltwater crocodile") that prey on large mammals, such as [buffalo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bovinae "Bovinae"), [deer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deer "Deer") and [wild boar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wild_boar "Wild boar"), diet shows great diversity. Diet is also greatly affected by the size and age of the individual within the same species. All young crocodiles hunt mostly [invertebrates](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Invertebrates "Invertebrates") and small [fish](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fish "Fish"), gradually moving on to larger prey. Being [ectothermic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ectotherm "Ectotherm") (cold-blooded) predators, they have a very slow [metabolism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metabolism "Metabolism"), so they can survive long periods without food. Despite their appearance of being slow, crocodiles have a very fast strike and are top [predators](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Predator "Predator") in their environment, and various species have been observed attacking and killing other [predators](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Predator "Predator") such as [sharks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shark "Shark") and [big cats](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_cat "Big cat").[\[88\]](#cite_note-NGeographicCroc-94)[\[89\]](#cite_note-95) Crocodiles are also known to be aggressive [scavengers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scavenger "Scavenger") who feed upon [carrion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carrion "Carrion") and steal from other predators.[\[90\]](#cite_note-96) Evidence suggests that crocodiles also feed upon fruits, based on the discovery of seeds in stools and stomachs from many subjects as well as accounts of them feeding.[\[91\]](#cite_note-97)[\[92\]](#cite_note-98)

Crocodiles have the most acidic stomach of any vertebrate. They can easily digest bones, hooves and horns. The [BBC TV](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/BBC_TV "BBC TV")[\[93\]](#cite_note-99) reported that a [Nile crocodile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nile_crocodile "Nile crocodile") that has lurked a long time underwater to catch prey builds up a large [oxygen debt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oxygen_debt "Oxygen debt"). When it has caught and eaten that prey, it closes its right [aortic arch](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aortic_arch "Aortic arch") and uses its left aortic arch to flush blood loaded with [carbon dioxide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_dioxide "Carbon dioxide") from its muscles directly to its stomach; the resulting excess acidity in its blood supply makes it much easier for the stomach lining to secrete more [stomach acid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stomach_acid "Stomach acid") to quickly dissolve bulks of swallowed prey flesh and bone. Many large crocodilians swallow stones (called gastroliths or stomach stones), which may act as ballast to balance their bodies or assist in crushing food,[\[58\]](#cite_note-ausfauna-64) similar to grit ingested by birds. [Herodotus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Herodotus "Herodotus") claimed that Nile crocodiles had a [symbiotic relationship](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Symbiosis "Symbiosis") with certain birds, such as the [Egyptian plover](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Egyptian_plover "Egyptian plover"), which enter the crocodile's mouth and pick [leeches](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leech "Leech") feeding on the crocodile's blood; with no evidence of this interaction actually occurring in any crocodile species, it is most likely mythical or allegorical fiction.[\[94\]](#cite_note-100)

#### Bite

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Nile_Crocodile_%28Crocodylus_niloticus%29_trying_to_swallow_a_big_Tilapia_%28Oreochromis_sp.%29..._%2816818888756%29.jpg/250px-Nile_Crocodile_%28Crocodylus_niloticus%29_trying_to_swallow_a_big_Tilapia_%28Oreochromis_sp.%29..._%2816818888756%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Nile_Crocodile_\(Crocodylus_niloticus\)_trying_to_swallow_a_big_Tilapia_\(Oreochromis_sp.\)..._\(16818888756\).jpg)

Nile crocodile trying to swallow a big [Tilapia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tilapia "Tilapia") in [Kruger National Park](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kruger_National_Park "Kruger National Park"), South Africa

Since they feed by grabbing and holding onto their prey, they have [evolved](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution "Evolution") sharp teeth for piercing and holding onto flesh, and powerful muscles to close the jaws and hold them shut. The teeth are not well-suited to tearing flesh off of large prey items as are the dentition and claws of many mammalian carnivores, the hooked bills and talons of [raptorial birds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bird_of_prey "Bird of prey"), or the serrated teeth of sharks. However, this is an advantage rather than a disadvantage to the crocodile since the properties of the teeth allow it to hold onto prey with the least possibility of the prey animal escaping. Cutting teeth, combined with the exceptionally high [bite force](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bite "Bite"), would pass through flesh easily enough to leave an escape opportunity for prey. The jaws can bite down with immense force, by far the strongest bite of any animal. The force of a large crocodile's bite is more than 5,000 lbf (22,000 N), which was measured in a 5.5 m (18 ft) [Nile crocodile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nile_crocodile "Nile crocodile"), in the field;[\[95\]](#cite_note-101) comparing to 335 lbf (1,490 N) for a [Rottweiler](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rottweiler "Rottweiler"), 800 lbf (3,600 N) for a [hyena](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hyena "Hyena"), 2,200 lbf (9,800 N) for an [American alligator](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_alligator "American alligator"),[\[96\]](#cite_note-102)\[_[failed verification](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability "Wikipedia:Verifiability")_\] and 4,095 lbf (18,220 N) for the largest confirmed [great white shark](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_white_shark "Great white shark").[\[97\]](#cite_note-GWB-103)

A 4.59 m (15.1 ft) long saltwater crocodile has been confirmed as having the strongest [bite force](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bite "Bite") ever recorded for an animal in a laboratory setting. It was able to apply a bite force value of 3,690 lbf (16,400 N), and thus surpassed the previous record of 2,125 lbf (9,450 N) made by a 3.9 m (13 ft) long [American alligator](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_alligator "American alligator").[\[41\]](#cite_note-bite-47)[\[98\]](#cite_note-lappin-104) Based on regression of mean body mass versus mean bite force, the bite force of a 6.7 m (22 ft) saltwater crocodile with an estimated mass of 1,308 kg (2,884 lb) was estimated at between 6,187 lbf (27,520 N) and 7,736 lbf (34,410 N).[\[41\]](#cite_note-bite-47) The study, led by Dr. [Gregory M. Erickson](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gregory_M._Erickson "Gregory M. Erickson"), also shed light on the larger, [extinct](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extinct "Extinct") species of [crocodilians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crocodilian "Crocodilian"). Since crocodile [anatomy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anatomy "Anatomy") has barely changed over the last 200 million years,[\[99\]](#cite_note-105) current data on modern crocodilians can be used to estimate the bite force of extinct species. An 11-to-12-metre (36–39 ft) _[Deinosuchus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deinosuchus "Deinosuchus")_ would apply a force of 23,100 lbf (103,000 N), nearly twice that of the latest, higher bite force estimations of _[Tyrannosaurus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tyrannosaurus "Tyrannosaurus")_ (12,814 lbf (57,000 N)).[\[45\]](#cite_note-nationalgeographic1-51)[\[100\]](#cite_note-FalkinghamBatesBiteforce2012-106)[\[101\]](#cite_note-BatesFalkingham2012-107)[\[102\]](#cite_note-108) The extraordinary bite of crocodilians is a result of their [anatomy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anatomy "Anatomy"). The space for the jaw muscle in the [skull](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Skull "Skull") is very large, which is easily visible from the outside as a bulge at each side. The [muscle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muscle "Muscle") is so stiff, it is almost as hard as bone to touch, as if it were the continuum of the skull. Another trait is that most of the muscle in a crocodile's jaw is arranged for clamping down. Despite the strong muscles to close the jaw, crocodiles have extremely small and weak muscles to open the jaw. Crocodiles can thus be subdued for study or transport by [taping](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Duct_tape "Duct tape") their jaws or holding their jaws shut with large [rubber bands](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rubber_band "Rubber band") cut from automobile [inner tubes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tire "Tire").

### Locomotion

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Crocodile_farm_in_Mexico.JPG/250px-Crocodile_farm_in_Mexico.JPG)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Crocodile_farm_in_Mexico.JPG)

A crocodile, in a farm, gaping to thermoregulate

Crocodiles can move quickly over short distances, even out of water. The [land speed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Land_speed "Land speed") record for a crocodile is 17 km/h (11 mph) measured in a galloping [Australian freshwater crocodile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Freshwater_crocodile "Freshwater crocodile").[\[103\]](#cite_note-109) Maximum speed varies between species. Some species can gallop, including Cuban crocodiles, Johnston's crocodiles, New Guinea crocodiles, [African dwarf crocodiles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dwarf_crocodile "Dwarf crocodile"), and even small [Nile crocodiles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nile_crocodiles "Nile crocodiles"). The fastest means by which most species can move is a "belly run", in which the body moves in a snake-like (sinusoidal) fashion, limbs splayed out to either side paddling away frantically while the tail whips to and fro. Crocodiles can reach speeds of 10–11 km/h (6–7 mph) when they "belly run", and often faster if slipping down muddy riverbanks. When a crocodile walks quickly, it holds its legs in a straighter and more upright position under its body, which is called the "high walk". This walk allows a speed of up to 5 km/h.[\[104\]](#cite_note-110)

Crocodiles may possess a [homing instinct](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Homing_instinct "Homing instinct"). In northern Australia, three rogue saltwater crocodiles were relocated 400 km (249 mi) by [helicopter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Helicopter "Helicopter"), but returned to their original locations within three weeks, based on data obtained from tracking devices attached to them.[\[105\]](#cite_note-111)

### Longevity

Measuring crocodile age is unreliable, although several techniques are used to derive a reasonable guess. The most common method is to measure lamellar growth rings in bones and teeth—each ring corresponds to a change in growth rate which typically occurs once a year between dry and wet seasons.[\[106\]](#cite_note-112) Bearing these inaccuracies in mind, it can be safely said that all crocodile species have an average lifespan of at least 30–40 years, and in the case of larger species an average of 60–70 years. The oldest crocodiles appear to be the largest species. [_C. porosus_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saltwater_crocodile "Saltwater crocodile") is estimated to live around 70 years on average, with limited evidence of some individuals exceeding 100 years.[\[107\]](#cite_note-113)

In captivity, some individuals are claimed to have lived for over a century. A male crocodile lived to an estimated age of 110–115 years in a Russian zoo in [Yekaterinburg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yekaterinburg "Yekaterinburg").[\[108\]](#cite_note-yeka-114) Named Kolya, he joined the zoo around 1913 to 1915, fully grown, after touring in an animal show, and lived until 1995.[\[108\]](#cite_note-yeka-114) A male freshwater crocodile lived to an estimated age of 120–140 years at the [Australia Zoo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australia_Zoo "Australia Zoo").[\[109\]](#cite_note-campbell2010-115) Known affectionately as "Mr. Freshie", he was rescued around 1970 by [Bob Irwin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bob_Irwin "Bob Irwin") and [Steve Irwin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Steve_Irwin "Steve Irwin"), after being shot twice by hunters and losing an eye as a result, and lived until 2010.[\[109\]](#cite_note-campbell2010-115) Crocworld Conservation Centre, in [Scottburgh](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scottburgh "Scottburgh"), South Africa, claims to have a male [Nile crocodile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nile_crocodile "Nile crocodile") that was born in 1900. Named Henry, the crocodile is said to have lived in [Botswana](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Botswana "Botswana") along the [Okavango River](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Okavango_River "Okavango River"), according to centre director Martin Rodrigues.[\[110\]](#cite_note-116)[\[111\]](#cite_note-117)

### Social behaviour and vocalisation

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Crocodiles_resting_together.jpeg/250px-Crocodiles_resting_together.jpeg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Crocodiles_resting_together.jpeg)

Captive crocodiles resting together with open jaws.

Crocodiles are the most social of reptiles. Even though they do not form social groups, many species congregate in certain sections of [rivers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/River "River"), tolerating each other at times of [feeding](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Feeding "Feeding") and [basking](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Body_temperature "Body temperature"). Most species are not highly territorial, with the exception of the saltwater crocodile, which is a highly [territorial](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Territory_\(animal\) "Territory (animal)") and aggressive species: a mature, male saltwater crocodile will not tolerate any other males at any time of the year, but most other species are more flexible. There is a certain form of [hierarchy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hierarchy "Hierarchy") in crocodiles: the largest and heaviest males are at the top, having access to the best basking site, while females are priority during a group feeding of a big kill or carcass. A good example of the hierarchy in crocodiles would be the case of the [Nile crocodile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nile_crocodile "Nile crocodile"). This species clearly displays all of these behaviours. Studies in this area are not thorough, however, and many species are yet to be studied in greater detail.[\[112\]](#cite_note-118) [Mugger crocodiles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mugger_crocodile "Mugger crocodile") are also known to show toleration in group feedings and tend to congregate in certain areas. However, males of all species are aggressive towards each other during mating season, to gain access to females.

Crocodilians are the most vocal of all reptiles, producing a wide variety of sounds depending on species, age, size and sex. Depending on the context, some species can communicate over 20 different messages through [vocalisations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Animal_communication "Animal communication") alone.[\[113\]](#cite_note-119) Some of these vocalisations are made during social communication, especially during [territorial](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Territory_\(animal\) "Territory (animal)") displays towards the same sex and [courtship](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Courtship_display "Courtship display") with the opposite sex; the common concern being [reproduction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reproduction "Reproduction"). Therefore most [conspecific](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conspecific "Conspecific") vocalisation is made during the [breeding season](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Breeding_season "Breeding season"), with the exception being year-round [territorial behaviour](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Territorial_behaviour "Territorial behaviour") in some species and quarrels during feeding. Crocodiles also produce different distress calls and in aggressive displays to their own kind and other animals; notably other predators during [interspecific](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Interspecific_competition "Interspecific competition") predatory confrontations over carcasses and terrestrial kills.

Specific vocalisations include —

-   **Chirp**: When about to hatch, the young make a "peeping" noise, which encourages the female to excavate the nest. The female then gathers the hatchlings in her mouth and transports them to the water, where they remain in a group for several months, protected by the female[\[114\]](#cite_note-120)
-   **Distress call**: A high-pitched call used mostly by younger animals to alert other crocodiles to imminent danger or an animal being attacked.
-   **Threat call**: A hissing sound that has also been described as a coughing noise.
-   **Hatching call**: Emitted by a female when breeding to alert other crocodiles that she has laid eggs in her nest.
-   **Bellowing**: Male crocodiles are especially vociferous. Bellowing choruses occur most often in the spring when breeding groups congregate, but can occur at any time of year. To bellow, males noticeably inflate as they raise the tail and head out of water, slowly waving the tail back and forth. They then puff out the throat and with a closed mouth, begin to vibrate air. Just before bellowing, males project an [infrasonic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Infrasonic "Infrasonic") signal at about 10 Hz through the water, which vibrates the ground and nearby objects. These low-frequency vibrations travel great distances through both air and water to advertise the male's presence and are so powerful they result in the water's appearing to "dance".[\[115\]](#cite_note-121)

### Reproduction

#### Mating

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Nile_crocodile_eggs.jpg/250px-Nile_crocodile_eggs.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Nile_crocodile_eggs.jpg)

Crocodile eggs

[Courtship](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Courtship_display "Courtship display") takes place in a series of behavioural interactions that include a variety of snout rubbing and submissive display that can take a long time. Mating always takes place in water, where the pair can be observed mating several times.[\[116\]](#cite_note-autogenerated2000-122)

#### Egg-laying & nesting

[Egg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Egg "Egg")\-laying usually takes place at night and about 30–40 minutes, which are laid in either holes or mound [nests](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nest "Nest"), depending on species.[\[116\]](#cite_note-autogenerated2000-122) The eggs are hard shelled, but translucent at the time of egg-laying. Depending on the species of crocodile, 7 to 95 eggs are laid.[\[117\]](#cite_note-webb-123) [Scutes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scute "Scute") may play a role in calcium storage for eggshell formation.[\[61\]](#cite_note-:0-67)

[Nesting](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nest "Nest") periods range from a few weeks up to six months. A hole nest is usually excavated in sand and a mound nest is usually constructed out of vegetation. Females can build or dig several trial nests which appear incomplete and abandoned later.[\[116\]](#cite_note-autogenerated2000-122) Females are highly protective of their nests and young. Crocodile [embryos](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Embryo "Embryo") do not have sex chromosomes, and unlike humans, sex is not determined genetically. [Sex is determined by temperature](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Temperature-dependent_sex_determination "Temperature-dependent sex determination"), where at 30 °C (86 °F) or less most hatchlings are females and at 31 °C (88 °F), offspring are of both sexes. A temperature of 32 to 33 °C (90 to 91 °F) gives mostly males whereas above 33 °C (91 °F) in some species continues to give males, but in other species resulting in females, which are sometimes called high-temperature females.[\[117\]](#cite_note-webb-123) Temperature also affects growth and survival rate of the young, which may explain the [sexual dimorphism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism "Sexual dimorphism") in crocodiles. The average [incubation period](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Incubation_period "Incubation period") is around 80 days, and also is dependent on temperature and species that usually ranges from 65 to 95 days. The eggshell structure is very conservative through evolution but there are enough changes to tell different species apart by their eggshell microstructure.[\[118\]](#cite_note-124)

At the time of hatching, the young start calling within the eggs. Hearing the calls, the female usually excavates the nest and sometimes takes the unhatched eggs in her mouth, slowly rolling the eggs to help the process. The young have an egg-tooth at the tip of their snouts, which is developed from the skin, and that helps them pierce out of the shell. Once hatched, the young are usually carried to the water in the female's mouth. She will then introduce her hatchlings to the water and even feed them.[\[119\]](#cite_note-125) The mother takes care of her young for over a year before the next mating season. In the absence of the mother crocodile, the father will act in her place to take care of the young.[\[120\]](#cite_note-126) However, even with sophisticated [parental nurturing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paternal_care "Paternal care"), young crocodiles have a very high mortality rate due to their vulnerability to predation.[\[121\]](#cite_note-127) A group of [hatchlings](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hatchling "Hatchling") is called a pod or [crèche](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cr%C3%A8che_\(zoology\) "Crèche (zoology)").[\[116\]](#cite_note-autogenerated2000-122)

### Cognition

Crocodiles possess some advanced cognitive abilities.[\[122\]](#cite_note-LA-128)[\[123\]](#cite_note-Wash-129) Crocodiles [cooperatively hunt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cooperative_hunting "Cooperative hunting").[\[124\]](#cite_note-One-130) Large numbers of crocodiles swim in circles to trap fish and take turns snatching them. In hunting larger prey, crocodiles swarm in, with one holding the prey down as the others rip it apart.

[Vladimir Dinets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Dinets "Vladimir Dinets") of the [University of Tennessee](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_Tennessee "University of Tennessee"), observing crocodile's use of [twigs as bait](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tool_use_by_animals#In_reptiles "Tool use by animals") was inconclusive.[\[125\]](#cite_note-131)

## Relationship with humans

### Danger to humans

Main article: [Crocodile attacks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crocodile_attacks "Crocodile attacks")

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Marine_Stingers_Sign_Cairns.JPG/250px-Marine_Stingers_Sign_Cairns.JPG)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Marine_Stingers_Sign_Cairns.JPG)

Crocodile warning sign, [Trinity Beach, Queensland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trinity_Beach,_Queensland "Trinity Beach, Queensland"), Australia

The larger species of crocodiles are very dangerous to humans, mainly because of their ability to strike before the person can react.[\[126\]](#cite_note-iucncsg-132) The [saltwater crocodile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saltwater_crocodile "Saltwater crocodile") and [Nile crocodile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nile_crocodile "Nile crocodile") are the most dangerous, killing hundreds of people each year in parts of Southeast Asia and Africa.[\[127\]](#cite_note-IUCN_Attacks-133) The [mugger crocodile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mugger_crocodile "Mugger crocodile") and [American crocodile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_crocodile "American crocodile") are also dangerous to humans.[\[128\]](#cite_note-Sideleau2023-134)

### Crocodile products

Further information: [Crocodile farm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crocodile_farm "Crocodile farm") and [Crocodile skin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crocodile_skin "Crocodile skin")

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Crocodile_wallets.jpg/250px-Crocodile_wallets.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Crocodile_wallets.jpg)

Crocodile leather wallets from a [Bangkok](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bangkok "Bangkok") crocodile farm

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Teriyaki_CrocTail.jpg/250px-Teriyaki_CrocTail.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Teriyaki_CrocTail.jpg)

A plate of crocodile meat in [teriyaki sauce](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Teriyaki_sauce "Teriyaki sauce") in [Helsinki](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Helsinki "Helsinki"), Finland

Crocodiles are protected in many parts of the world, but are also farmed commercially. Their hides are tanned and used to make leather goods such as shoes and [handbags](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Handbag "Handbag"); crocodile meat is also considered a delicacy.[\[129\]](#cite_note-135) The most commonly farmed species are the saltwater and Nile crocodiles, while a hybrid of the saltwater and the rare [Siamese crocodile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Siamese_crocodile "Siamese crocodile") is also bred in Asian farms. Farming has resulted in an increase in the saltwater crocodile population in Australia, as eggs are usually harvested from the wild, so landowners have an incentive to conserve their habitat. Crocodile leather can be made into goods such as wallets, briefcases, purses, handbags, belts, hats, and shoes. [Crocodile oil](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crocodile_oil "Crocodile oil") has been used for various purposes.[\[130\]](#cite_note-136) [Crocodiles were eaten by Vietnamese](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vietnamese_cuisine#Exotic_dishes "Vietnamese cuisine") while they were taboo and off limits for Chinese. Vietnamese women who married Chinese men adopted the Chinese taboo.[\[131\]](#cite_note-Peters2012-137)

Crocodile meat is consumed in some countries, such as Australia, [Ethiopia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ethiopia "Ethiopia"), Thailand, South Africa, China, and Cuba (in pickled form). It is also occasionally eaten as an "exotic" delicacy in the western world.[\[132\]](#cite_note-138) Cuts of meat include backstrap and tail fillet.\[_[citation needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")_\]

Due to high demand for crocodile products, [TRAFFIC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Traffic_\(conservation_programme\) "Traffic (conservation programme)") states that 1,418,487 Nile Crocodile skins were exported from Africa between 2006 and 2015.[\[133\]](#cite_note-139)

### Crocodile hunting and conservation

[Aboriginal Australians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aboriginal_Australians "Aboriginal Australians") harvested eggs and hunted crocodiles in a sustainable way for many thousands of years. The [Brinkin people](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brinkin_people "Brinkin people") (aka Marrithiyal) of the [Daly River](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Daly_River_\(Northern_Territory\) "Daly River (Northern Territory)") in the [Northern Territory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Northern_Territory "Northern Territory") (NT) used [harpoons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Harpoon "Harpoon") and [bamboo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bamboo "Bamboo"), and even their own hands to capture crocodiles for food.[\[134\]](#cite_note-mash-140) After settlement of northern Australia, in the late-19th and early 20th centuries, non-[Indigenous](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indigenous_Australians "Indigenous Australians") people killed individual crocodiles, mostly by locals to protect the population, or novelty-seeking visitors, or just opportunistically, so numbers were not noticeably reduced. From the 1930s, commercial hunting began, with Aboriginal people often employed to kill the crocodiles using traditional methods. From the 1940s to the 1960s, hunting began on a larger scale using [.303 rifles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/.303_rifle ".303 rifle").[\[135\]](#cite_note-qha-141) They were hunted for leather, with the skins shipped to plants in capital cities.[\[134\]](#cite_note-mash-140) [Western Australia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Western_Australia "Western Australia") banned hunting freshwater crocodiles in 1962 and saltwater crocodiles in 1970, while NT bans were brought in 1964 and 1971; [Queensland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Queensland "Queensland") did not pass such legislation. The federal government later banned the export of crocodile skins, which brought commercial hunting to an end in Queensland.[\[135\]](#cite_note-qha-141) They have been a [protected species](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Protected_species "Protected species") since the 1970s, when numbers were down to approximately 3,000 in the NT at the lowest estimate. In 2021, after several attacks on humans by the "salties" and an estimated population of around 200,000 had been reached, Queensland politician [Bob Katter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bob_Katter "Bob Katter") called for the reintroduction of hunting.[\[136\]](#cite_note-vidler2021-142)

### In religion and mythology

Further information: [Crocodilia § Cultural depictions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crocodilia#Cultural_depictions "Crocodilia")

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Sobek_Oxford.jpg/250px-Sobek_Oxford.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Sobek_Oxford.jpg)

Statue of [Sobek](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sobek "Sobek") from the mortuary temple of [Amenemhat III](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amenemhat_III "Amenemhat III") c. 1810 BC. [Ashmolean Museum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ashmolean_Museum "Ashmolean Museum"), Oxford.

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Plaque_with_Crocodile_Deity%2C_ca._700-900.%2C33.448.12.jpg/250px-Plaque_with_Crocodile_Deity%2C_ca._700-900.%2C33.448.12.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Plaque_with_Crocodile_Deity,_ca._700-900.,33.448.12.jpg)

Brooklyn Museum – Plaque from Cocle, Panama, with Crocodile Deity, ca. 700–900.,33.448.12

Crocodiles have appeared in various forms in religions across the world. [Ancient Egypt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancient_Egypt "Ancient Egypt") had [Sobek](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sobek "Sobek"), the crocodile-headed god, with his cult-city [Crocodilopolis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crocodilopolis "Crocodilopolis"), as well as [Taweret](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taweret "Taweret"), the goddess of childbirth and fertility, with the back and tail of a crocodile.[\[137\]](#cite_note-143) The [Jukun shrine in the Wukari Federation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wukari_Federation#Religion "Wukari Federation"), Nigeria is dedicated to crocodiles in thanks for their aid during migration.[\[138\]](#cite_note-144) In [Madagascar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Madagascar "Madagascar") various peoples such as the [Sakalava](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sakalava_people "Sakalava people") and [Antandroy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antandroy "Antandroy") see crocodiles as ancestor spirits and under local _[fady](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fady_\(taboo\) "Fady (taboo)")_ often offer them food;[\[139\]](#cite_note-Campbell,_Gwyn_2012-145)[\[140\]](#cite_note-146) in the case of the latter at least a crocodile features prominently as an ancestor deity.[\[139\]](#cite_note-Campbell,_Gwyn_2012-145)[\[141\]](#cite_note-147)

Crocodiles appear in different forms in [Hinduism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hinduism "Hinduism"). [Varuna](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Varuna "Varuna"), a [Vedic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Historical_Vedic_religion "Historical Vedic religion") and Hindu god, rides a part-crocodile [makara](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Makara_\(Hindu_mythology\) "Makara (Hindu mythology)"); his consort [Varuni](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Varuni "Varuni") rides a crocodile.[\[124\]](#cite_note-One-130) Similarly the goddess personifications of the [Ganga](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ganges "Ganges") and [Yamuna](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yamuna "Yamuna") rivers are often depicted as riding crocodiles.[\[142\]](#cite_note-148)[\[143\]](#cite_note-149)[\[144\]](#cite_note-150) Also in India, in [Goa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Goa "Goa"), crocodile worship is practised, including the annual _Mannge Thapnee_ ceremony.[\[145\]](#cite_note-151)

[Sikh](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sikh "Sikh") warriors known as [nihang](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nihang "Nihang") also have connections with crocodiles. _Nihang_ may come from the Persian word for a mythical sea creature ([Persian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Persian_language "Persian language"): نهنگ).[\[146\]](#cite_note-152) The term owes its origin to [Mughal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mughal_Empire "Mughal Empire") historians, who compared the ferocity of the Akali with that of crocodiles.[\[147\]](#cite_note-153)[\[148\]](#cite_note-154) In [Sikhism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sikhism "Sikhism") however, _Akali_ refers to the immortal army of Akal (god).[\[149\]](#cite_note-155)

In Latin America, [Cipactli](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cipactli "Cipactli") was the giant earth crocodile of the [Aztec](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aztec "Aztec") and other [Nahua peoples](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nahua_peoples "Nahua peoples").\[_[citation needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")_\]

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/City_of_Surabaya_Logo.svg/250px-City_of_Surabaya_Logo.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:City_of_Surabaya_Logo.svg)

Fighting shark and crocodile, the emblem of Surabaya

The name of [Surabaya](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surabaya "Surabaya"), Indonesia, is locally believed to be derived from the words "_suro"_ ([shark](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shark "Shark")) and "_boyo"_ (crocodile), two creatures which, in a local [myth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mythology "Mythology"), fought each other in order to gain the title of "the strongest and most powerful animal" in the area. It was said that the two powerful animals agreed for a truce and set boundaries; that the shark's domain would be in the sea while the crocodile's domain would be on the land. However one day the shark swam into the river estuary to hunt; this angered the crocodile, who declared it his territory. The Shark argued that the river was a water-realm which meant that it was shark territory, while the crocodile argued that the river flowed deep inland, so it was therefore crocodile territory. The two animals bit each other and a ferocious fight ensued. Finally the shark, badly bitten, fled to the open sea, and in the end the crocodile ruled the estuarine area that today is the city.[\[150\]](#cite_note-Irwan_Rouf_&_Shenia_Ananda_60-156) Another source alludes to a [Jayabaya](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jayabaya "Jayabaya") prophecy—a 12th-century psychic king of [Kediri Kingdom](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kediri_Kingdom "Kediri Kingdom")—as he foresaw a fight between a giant [white shark](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/White_shark "White shark") and a giant white crocodile taking place in the area. This is sometimes interpreted as a foretelling of the [Mongol invasion of Java](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mongol_invasion_of_Java "Mongol invasion of Java"), a major conflict between the forces of the [Kublai Khan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kublai_Khan "Kublai Khan"), [Mongol](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mongol_Empire "Mongol Empire") ruler of [China](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/China "China"), and those of [Raden Wijaya](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Raden_Wijaya "Raden Wijaya")'s [Majapahit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Majapahit "Majapahit") in 1293.[\[151\]](#cite_note-EastJava-157) The two animals are now used as the city's symbol, with the two facing and circling each other, as depicted in a statue appropriately located near the entrance to the [city zoo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surabaya_Zoo "Surabaya Zoo") (see photo on the [Surabaya](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surabaya "Surabaya") page).\[_[citation needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")_\]

### In language and as symbols

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/The_American_River_Ganges_%28Thomas_Nast_cartoon%29.jpg/250px-The_American_River_Ganges_%28Thomas_Nast_cartoon%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:The_American_River_Ganges_\(Thomas_Nast_cartoon\).jpg)

1876 U.S. cartoon by [Thomas Nast](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thomas_Nast "Thomas Nast") depicting Roman Catholic bishops as crocodiles attacking public schools, with the connivance of Irish Catholic politicians

Main articles: [Crocodile tears](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crocodile_tears "Crocodile tears") and [Crocodile (politics)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crocodile_\(politics\) "Crocodile (politics)")

The term "[crocodile tears](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crocodile_tears "Crocodile tears")" (and equivalents in other languages) refers to a false, insincere display of emotion, such as a [hypocrite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hypocrite "Hypocrite") crying fake tears of [grief](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Grief "Grief"). It is derived from an ancient anecdote that crocodiles weep in order to lure their prey, or that they [cry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crying "Crying") for the victims they are eating, first told in the _[Bibliotheca](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bibliotheca_\(Photius\) "Bibliotheca (Photius)")_ by [Photios I of Constantinople](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Photios_I_of_Constantinople "Photios I of Constantinople").[\[152\]](#cite_note-158) The story is repeated in bestiaries such as [_De bestiis et aliis rebus_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_medieval_bestiaries#DeBestiis "List of medieval bestiaries"). This tale was first spread widely in English in the stories of the _Travels of [Sir John Mandeville](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sir_John_Mandeville "Sir John Mandeville")_ in the 14th century, and appears in several of [Shakespeare](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shakespeare "Shakespeare")'s plays.[\[153\]](#cite_note-159) In fact, crocodiles can and do generate tears, but they do not actually cry.[\[154\]](#cite_note-160)

In the UK, a row of [schoolchildren](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Schoolchildren "Schoolchildren") walking in pairs, or two by two is known as "crocodile".[\[155\]](#cite_note-161)[\[156\]](#cite_note-162)

In politics, a crocodile has been used in different countries to describe [politicians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Politician "Politician") and [political groups](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parliamentary_group "Parliamentary group"), sometimes as a compliment for a leader seen as strong and strategic, while in others it is a negative label linked to [corruption](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Corruption "Corruption"), [greed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greed "Greed"), or [predatory behavior](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Predation "Predation").[\[157\]](#cite_note-163)[\[158\]](#cite_note-164)

### Fashion logos

The French clothing company [Lacoste](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lacoste "Lacoste") features a crocodile in its [logo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Logo "Logo"). The American shoe company [Crocs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crocs "Crocs") also uses this imagery in its logo.[\[159\]](#cite_note-165)

141.  **[^](#cite_ref-147 "Jump up")** An _[Athenæum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Athenaeum_\(British_magazine\) "The Athenaeum (British magazine)")_ article [makes a claim](https://books.google.com/books?id=-t8RAAAAYAAJ&pg=PA247) of a specific crocodile deity named textually as "Jacaret", but less biased sources do not mention this theonym in any capacity.
142.  **[^](#cite_ref-148 "Jump up")** ["Holy Rivers, Lakes, and Oceans"](https://web.archive.org/web/20141014184216/http://hinduism.iskcon.org/practice/504.htm). _Heart of Hinduism_. ISKCON Educational Services. 2004. Archived from [the original](http://hinduism.iskcon.org/practice/504.htm) on 14 October 2014. Retrieved 16 September 2014. Most rivers are considered female and are personified as goddesses. Ganga, who features in the Mahabharata, is usually shown riding on a crocodile (see right).
143.  **[^](#cite_ref-149 "Jump up")** Kumar, Nitin (August 2003). ["Ganga The River Goddess – Tales in Art and Mythology"](http://www.exoticindiaart.com/article/ganga/). The second distinguishing aspect of Ganga's iconography is her animal mount, which is often shown serving as a pedestal for her. This is the makara, a hybrid creature having the body of a crocodile and the tail of a fish. The makara in Hindu thought corresponds to the star sign of Capricorn in western astrology. The crocodile is a unique animal in that it can live on both land and sea. It thus denotes the wisdom of both the earth and waters.
144.  **[^](#cite_ref-150 "Jump up")** ["Hindu gods and their holy mounts"](https://web.archive.org/web/20140623165912/http://svzoo.org/html/anicult2.htm). Sri.Venkateswara Zoological Park. Archived from [the original](http://svzoo.org/html/anicult2.htm) on 23 June 2014. The river goddesses, Ganga and Yamuna, were appropriately mounted on a tortoise and a crocodile respectively.

January–March 1995. [Archived](https://ghostarchive.org/archive/20221009/http://www.iucncsg.org/365_docs/attachments/protarea/CSG%20-96e3654b.pdf) (PDF) from the original on 9 October 2022.

## Notes

1.  **[^](#cite_ref-5 "Jump up")** The Ancient Greek words for _croc._ were: σοῦχος[\[2\]](#cite_note-tuftscroc.-2) which was the Ancient Egyptian _croc._[\[3\]](#cite_note-3) and,[\[2\]](#cite_note-tuftscroc.-2) probably,[\[4\]](#cite_note-LSJcroc.-4) ὀδοντοτύραννος[\[2\]](#cite_note-tuftscroc.-2) the Ancient Indian _croc._[\[4\]](#cite_note-LSJcroc.-4)
2.  **[^](#cite_ref-13 "Jump up")** It has been suggested,[\[6\]](#cite_note-Wylie1780231237-7)[\[5\]](#cite_note-10.1093-6)[\[7\]](#cite_note-Russini-8)[\[8\]](#cite_note-Aleksic-9)[\[9\]](#cite_note-Ikram-10) that the word parts _croc._ & _dile_ are compounded from _krokè_ ('pebbles'), and _drilos[\[5\]](#cite_note-10.1093-6)[\[8\]](#cite_note-Aleksic-9)[\[9\]](#cite_note-Ikram-10) / deilos[\[6\]](#cite_note-Wylie1780231237-7)[\[7\]](#cite_note-Russini-8)_ ('worm'),[\[6\]](#cite_note-Wylie1780231237-7)[\[5\]](#cite_note-10.1093-6)[\[7\]](#cite_note-Russini-8)[\[8\]](#cite_note-Aleksic-9)[\[9\]](#cite_note-Ikram-10) although the former is only attested as a "a stripling" a colloquialism for a young man, or, a circumcised man[\[10\]](#cite_note-11) the latter in ancient sources in fact is interpreted as: cowardly, vile, worthless, lowborn, mean[\[11\]](#cite_note-12)
3.  **[^](#cite_ref-23 "Jump up")** The croc. in question was an Indian croc. The text tells the tale of [Alexander of Macedonia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_of_Macedonia "Alexander of Macedonia"):
    
    Weber p.226 - "Alexander returns into [Upper India](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Upper_India "Upper India") where he defeats the inhabitants - The host attacked by a monstrous beast, and by elephants," (text form: _olifauntz_, line 5734) "which are subdued";
    
    commences line 5688:
    
    The kynge thennes went forth,
    
    Ayein into Ynde in the north,
    
    That is y-cleped, als I fynde
    
    In the book, the vpper Ynde.
    
4.  **[^](#cite_ref-25 "Jump up")** The modern / current German term is: das Krokodil[\[21\]](#cite_note-24)
5.  **[^](#cite_ref-28 "Jump up")** Is shown in _Gessner_, 1560, Historiæ animalium p. 54 as "GALLICE Crocodile" (i.e. French[\[22\]](#cite_note-26)) - see: p.27:[\[23\]](#cite_note-27)
6.  **[^](#cite_ref-31 "Jump up")** _[PROSOPOPOIA.](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prosopopoia "Prosopopoia")[Or Mother Hubberds Tale.](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mother_Hubberd%27s_Tale "Mother Hubberd's Tale")_[\[25\]](#cite_note-EdSp-30)
    
    Then vnto him all monstrous beasts resorted
    
    Bred of two kindes, as Griffons, Minotaures,
    
    Crocodiles, Dragons, Beauers, and Centaures:
    
    With those himselfe he strengthned mightelie,
    
    That feare he neede no force of enemie.

Extant [Crocodilian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crocodilia "Crocodilia") species

| --- |
| 

-   Kingdom: [Animalia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Animal "Animal")
-   Phylum: [Chordata](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chordate "Chordate")

-   (unranked): [Archosauria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archosaur "Archosaur")
